<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;border-top:1px solid #aaa;padding-bottom:0;text-align:center;">Academic disciplines</div><div class="NavContent plainlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">

</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Labor" title="Template:Labor"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Labor" title="Template talk:Labor"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Labor&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Labour Party</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Centre-left_politics" title="Centre-left politics">centre-left</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_political_parties_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="List of political parties in the United Kingdom">political party in the United Kingdom</a>. It has been described as a <a href="/wiki/Big_tent" title="Big tent">broad church</a>, bringing together an alliance of <a href="/wiki/Social_democracy" title="Social democracy">social-democratic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Democratic_socialism" title="Democratic socialism">democratic socialist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">trade unionist</a> outlooks.<sup id="cite_ref-Worley2009_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Worley2009-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The party's platform emphasises greater state intervention, <a href="/wiki/Social_justice" title="Social justice">social justice</a> and strengthening workers' rights. Labour is a full member of the <a href="/wiki/Party_of_European_Socialists" title="Party of European Socialists">Party of European Socialists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Alliance" title="Progressive Alliance">Progressive Alliance</a>, and holds observer status in the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_International" title="Socialist International">Socialist International</a>. As of 2017, the party was considered the "largest party in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>" in terms of party membership, with more than half-a-million members.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Labour Party was founded in 1900, having grown out of the <a href="/wiki/Trade_union_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Trade union movement">trade union movement</a> and <a href="/wiki/Socialism_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Socialism in the United Kingdom">socialist</a> parties of the nineteenth century. It overtook the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a> to become the main opposition to the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> in the early 1920s, forming minority governments under <a href="/wiki/Ramsay_MacDonald" title="Ramsay MacDonald">Ramsay MacDonald</a> in <a href="/wiki/First_MacDonald_ministry" title="First MacDonald ministry">1924</a> and from <a href="/wiki/Second_MacDonald_ministry" title="Second MacDonald ministry">1929 to 1931</a>. Labour later served in the <a href="/wiki/Churchill_war_ministry" title="Churchill war ministry">wartime coalition</a> from 1940 to 1945, after which it formed a <a href="/wiki/Attlee_ministry" title="Attlee ministry">majority government</a> under <a href="/wiki/Clement_Attlee" title="Clement Attlee">Clement Attlee</a> until 1951. Labour was next in government from <a href="/wiki/First_Wilson_ministry" class="mw-redirect" title="First Wilson ministry">1964 to 1970</a> under <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a> and from <a href="/wiki/Labour_Government_1974%E2%80%9379" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour Government 1974–79">1974 to 1979</a>, first under Wilson and then under <a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a>.
</p><p>The Labour Party was most recently in government from <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1997" title="United Kingdom general election, 1997">1997</a> to <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2010" title="United Kingdom general election, 2010">2010</a> under <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a> during the "<a href="/wiki/New_Labour" title="New Labour">New Labour</a>" era, first elected with a landslide majority of 179, reduced to 66 in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2005" title="United Kingdom general election, 2005">2005</a> election. Since leaving government, the party has been led by <a href="/wiki/Ed_Miliband" title="Ed Miliband">Ed Miliband</a> from 2010 and <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Corbyn" title="Jeremy Corbyn">Jeremy Corbyn</a> from 2015.
</p><p>Labour is currently the <a href="/wiki/Official_Opposition_(United_Kingdom)" class="mw-redirect" title="Official Opposition (United Kingdom)">Official Opposition</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament of the United Kingdom</a>, having won the second-largest number of seats in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2017" title="United Kingdom general election, 2017">2017 general election</a>. The Labour Party is currently the largest party in the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_for_Wales" title="National Assembly for Wales">Welsh Assembly</a>, forming the main party in the current Welsh government. The party is also the third largest in the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a> and sends the most <a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_European_Parliament" title="Member of the European Parliament">MEPs</a> from the United Kingdom to the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a>, sitting in the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Alliance_of_Socialists_and_Democrats" title="Progressive Alliance of Socialists and Democrats">Socialists and Democrats</a> Group. Whilst the party organises in <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_in_Northern_Ireland" title="Labour Party in Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>, it does not contest elections there, instead supporting the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_and_Labour_Party" title="Social Democratic and Labour Party">Social Democratic and Labour Party</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Labour_Party_(UK)" title="History of the Labour Party (UK)">History of the Labour Party (UK)</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_socialist_movement_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="History of the socialist movement in the United Kingdom">History of the socialist movement in the United Kingdom</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Founding">Founding</span></h3>
<p>The Labour Party originated in the late 19th century, meeting the demand for a new political party to represent the interests and needs of the urban working class, a demographic which had increased in number, and many of whom only gained <a href="/wiki/Suffrage" title="Suffrage">suffrage</a> with the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1884" title="Representation of the People Act 1884">Representation of the People Act 1884</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Some members of the trades union movement became interested in moving into the political field, and after further extensions of the voting franchise in 1867 and 1885, the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a> endorsed some trade-union sponsored candidates. The first <a href="/wiki/Liberal-Labour_(UK)" title="Liberal-Labour (UK)">Lib–Lab</a> candidate to stand was <a href="/wiki/George_Odger" title="George Odger">George Odger</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Southwark_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Southwark (UK Parliament constituency)">Southwark</a> by-election of 1870. In addition, several small socialist groups had formed around this time, with the intention of linking the movement to political policies. Among these were the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Labour_Party" title="Independent Labour Party">Independent Labour Party</a>, the intellectual and largely middle-class <a href="/wiki/Fabian_Society" title="Fabian Society">Fabian Society</a>, the Marxist <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Federation" title="Social Democratic Federation">Social Democratic Federation</a><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Labour_Party_(1888%E2%80%931893)" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Labour Party (1888–1893)">Scottish Labour Party</a>.
</p><p>At the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1895" title="United Kingdom general election, 1895">1895 general election</a>, the Independent Labour Party put up 28 candidates but won only 44,325 votes. <a href="/wiki/Keir_Hardie" title="Keir Hardie">Keir Hardie</a>, the leader of the party, believed that to obtain success in parliamentary elections, it would be necessary to join with other left-wing groups. Hardie's roots as a lay preacher contributed to an ethos in the party which led to the comment by 1950s General Secretary <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Phillips" title="Morgan Phillips">Morgan Phillips</a> that "Socialism in Britain owed more to Methodism than Marx".<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Labour_Representation_Committee">Labour Representation Committee</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Labour_Representation_Committee_(1900)" title="Labour Representation Committee (1900)">Labour Representation Committee (1900)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jameskeirhardie.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Jameskeirhardie.jpg/170px-Jameskeirhardie.jpg" width="170" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b1/Jameskeirhardie.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="345" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jameskeirhardie.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Keir_Hardie" title="Keir Hardie">Keir Hardie</a>, one of the Labour Party's founders and its first leader</div></div></div>
<p>In 1899, a <a href="/wiki/Doncaster" title="Doncaster">Doncaster</a> member of the <a href="/wiki/Amalgamated_Society_of_Railway_Servants" title="Amalgamated Society of Railway Servants">Amalgamated Society of Railway Servants</a>, Thomas R. Steels, proposed in his union branch that the <a href="/wiki/Trade_Union_Congress" class="mw-redirect" title="Trade Union Congress">Trade Union Congress</a> call a special conference to bring together all left-wing organisations and form them into a single body that would sponsor Parliamentary candidates. The motion was passed at all stages by the TUC, and the proposed conference was held at the Memorial Hall on Farringdon Street on 26 and 27 February 1900. The meeting was attended by a broad spectrum of working-class and left-wing organisations—trades unions represented about one third of the membership of the TUC delegates.<sup id="cite_ref-socialisthistorysociety.co.uk_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-socialisthistorysociety.co.uk-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After a debate, the 129 delegates passed Hardie's motion to establish "a distinct Labour group in Parliament, who shall have their own whips, and agree upon their policy, which must embrace a readiness to cooperate with any party which for the time being may be engaged in promoting legislation in the direct interests of labour."<sup id="cite_ref-Collection_highlights_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collection_highlights-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> This created an association called the Labour Representation Committee (LRC), meant to co-ordinate attempts to support MPs sponsored by trade unions and represent the working-class population.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThorpe20088_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThorpe20088-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> It had no single leader, and in the absence of one, the Independent Labour Party nominee <a href="/wiki/Ramsay_MacDonald" title="Ramsay MacDonald">Ramsay MacDonald</a> was elected as Secretary. He had the difficult task of keeping the various strands of opinions in the LRC united. The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1900" title="United Kingdom general election, 1900">October 1900</a> "Khaki election" came too soon for the new party to campaign effectively; total expenses for the election only came to £33.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWrightCarter1997_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWrightCarter1997-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Only 15 candidatures were sponsored, but two were successful; <a href="/wiki/Keir_Hardie" title="Keir Hardie">Keir Hardie</a> in <a href="/wiki/Merthyr_Tydfil_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Merthyr Tydfil (UK Parliament constituency)">Merthyr Tydfil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Bell_(British_politician)" title="Richard Bell (British politician)">Richard Bell</a> in <a href="/wiki/Derby_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Derby (UK Parliament constituency)">Derby</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThorpe2001_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThorpe2001-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Support for the LRC was boosted by the 1901 <a href="/wiki/Taff_Vale_Case" class="mw-redirect" title="Taff Vale Case">Taff Vale Case</a>, a dispute between strikers and a railway company that ended with the union being ordered to pay £23,000 damages for a strike. The judgement effectively made strikes illegal since employers could recoup the cost of lost business from the unions. The apparent acquiescence of the Conservative Government of <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Balfour" title="Arthur Balfour">Arthur Balfour</a> to industrial and business interests (traditionally the allies of the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a> in opposition to the Conservative's landed interests) intensified support for the LRC against a government that appeared to have little concern for the industrial proletariat and its problems.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThorpe2001_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThorpe2001-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LabourPartyPlaque.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/LabourPartyPlaque.jpg/220px-LabourPartyPlaque.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/LabourPartyPlaque.jpg/330px-LabourPartyPlaque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/LabourPartyPlaque.jpg/440px-LabourPartyPlaque.jpg 2x" data-file-width="582" data-file-height="416" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LabourPartyPlaque.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Labour Party Plaque from Caroone House, 14 Farringdon Street</div></div></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1906" title="United Kingdom general election, 1906">1906 election</a>, the LRC won 29 seats—helped by a <a href="/wiki/Lib%E2%80%93Lab_pact" title="Lib–Lab pact">secret 1903 pact</a> between <a href="/wiki/Ramsay_MacDonald" title="Ramsay MacDonald">Ramsay MacDonald</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal</a> <a href="/wiki/Chief_Whip" title="Chief Whip">Chief Whip</a> <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Gladstone,_1st_Viscount_Gladstone" title="Herbert Gladstone, 1st Viscount Gladstone">Herbert Gladstone</a> that aimed to avoid splitting the opposition vote between Labour and Liberal candidates in the interest of removing the Conservatives from office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThorpe2001_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThorpe2001-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In their first meeting after the election the group's Members of Parliament decided to adopt the name "The Labour Party" formally (15 February 1906). Keir Hardie, who had taken a leading role in getting the party established, was elected as Chairman of the Parliamentary Labour Party (in effect, the Leader), although only by one vote over <a href="/wiki/David_Shackleton" title="David Shackleton">David Shackleton</a> after several ballots. In the party's early years the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Labour_Party" title="Independent Labour Party">Independent Labour Party</a> (ILP) provided much of its activist base as the party did not have individual membership until 1918 but operated as a conglomerate of affiliated bodies. The <a href="/wiki/Fabian_Society" title="Fabian Society">Fabian Society</a> provided much of the intellectual stimulus for the party. One of the first acts of the new Liberal Government was to reverse the Taff Vale judgement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThorpe2001_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThorpe2001-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/People%27s_History_Museum" title="People&#39;s History Museum">People's History Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a> holds the minutes of the first Labour Party meeting in 1906 and has them on display in the Main Galleries.<sup id="cite_ref-People&#39;s_History_Museum_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-People&#39;s_History_Museum-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Also within the museum is the Labour History Archive and Study Centre, which holds the collection of the Labour Party, with material ranging from 1900 to the present day.<sup id="cite_ref-phm.org.uk_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-phm.org.uk-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_December_1910" title="United Kingdom general election, December 1910">December 1910 election</a> saw 42 Labour MPs elected to the House of Commons, a significant victory since, a year before the election, the House of Lords had passed the <a href="/wiki/Osborne_judgment" class="mw-redirect" title="Osborne judgment">Osborne judgment</a> ruling that trade union members would have to 'opt in' to sending contributions to Labour, rather than their consent being presumed. The governing Liberals were unwilling to repeal this judicial decision with primary legislation. The height of Liberal compromise was to introduce a wage for Members of Parliament to remove the need to involve the trade unions. By 1913, faced with the opposition of the largest trade unions, the Liberal government passed the Trade Disputes Act to allow unions to fund Labour MPs once more without seeking the express consent of their members.
</p><p>During the First World War the Labour Party split between supporters and opponents of the conflict but opposition to the war grew within the party as time went on. <a href="/wiki/Ramsay_MacDonald" title="Ramsay MacDonald">Ramsay MacDonald</a>, a notable anti-war campaigner, resigned as leader of the Parliamentary Labour Party and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Henderson" title="Arthur Henderson">Arthur Henderson</a> became the main figure of authority within the party. He was soon accepted into <a href="/wiki/H._H._Asquith" title="H. H. Asquith">Prime Minister Asquith</a>'s war cabinet, becoming the first Labour Party member to serve in government.
</p><p>Despite mainstream Labour Party's support for the coalition the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Labour_Party" title="Independent Labour Party">Independent Labour Party</a> was instrumental in opposing conscription through organisations such as the <a href="/wiki/Non-Conscription_Fellowship" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-Conscription Fellowship">Non-Conscription Fellowship</a> while a Labour Party affiliate, the <a href="/wiki/British_Socialist_Party" title="British Socialist Party">British Socialist Party</a>, organised a number of unofficial strikes.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Arthur_Henderson" title="Arthur Henderson">Arthur Henderson</a> resigned from the Cabinet in 1917 amid calls for party unity to be replaced by <a href="/wiki/George_Nicoll_Barnes" class="mw-redirect" title="George Nicoll Barnes">George Barnes</a>. The growth in Labour's local activist base and organisation was reflected in the elections following the war, the <a href="/wiki/Co-operative" class="mw-redirect" title="Co-operative">co-operative</a> movement now providing its own resources to the <a href="/wiki/Co-operative_Party" title="Co-operative Party">Co-operative Party</a> after the armistice. The Co-operative Party later reached an electoral agreement with the Labour Party.
</p><p>Henderson turned his attention to building a strong constituency-based support network for the Labour Party. Previously, it had little national organisation, based largely on branches of unions and socialist societies. Working with Ramsay MacDonald and Sidney Webb, Henderson in 1918 established a national network of constituency organisations. They operated separately from trade unions and the National Executive Committee and were open to everyone sympathetic to the party's policies. Secondly, Henderson secured the adoption of a comprehensive statement of party policies, as drafted by <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Webb" class="mw-redirect" title="Sidney Webb">Sidney Webb</a>. Entitled "Labour and the New Social Order," it remained the basic Labour platform until 1950. It proclaimed a socialist party whose principless included a guaranteed minimum standard of living for everyone, nationalisation of industry, and heavy taxation of large incomes and of wealth.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> It was in 1918 that <a href="/wiki/Clause_IV" title="Clause IV">Clause IV</a>, as drafted by <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Webb" class="mw-redirect" title="Sidney Webb">Sidney Webb</a>, was adopted into Labour's constitution, committing the party to work towards "the common ownership of the means of production, distribution and exchange."
</p><p>With the <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1918" title="Representation of the People Act 1918">Representation of the People Act 1918</a>, almost all adult men (excepting only peers, criminals and lunatics) and most women over the age of thirty were given the right to vote, almost tripling the British electorate at a stroke, from 7.7 million in 1912 to 21.4 million in 1918. This set the scene for a surge in Labour representation in parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-Rosemary_Rees_2003_p._200_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rosemary_Rees_2003_p._200-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Great_Britain" title="Communist Party of Great Britain">Communist Party of Great Britain</a> was refused affiliation to the Labour Party between 1921 and 1923.<sup id="cite_ref-Glasgow_Digital_Library_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glasgow_Digital_Library-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a> declined rapidly, and the party also suffered a catastrophic split which allowed the Labour Party to gain much of the Liberals' support.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>  With the Liberals thus in disarray, Labour won 142 seats in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1922" title="United Kingdom general election, 1922">1922</a>, making it the second largest political group in the House of Commons and the <a href="/wiki/Official_Opposition_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="Official Opposition (UK)">official opposition</a> to the Conservative government. After the election Ramsay MacDonald was voted the first official <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_Kingdom_Labour_Party_leaders" class="mw-redirect" title="List of United Kingdom Labour Party leaders">leader of the Labour Party</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="First_Labour_government.2C_1924"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Labour_government,_1924">First Labour government, 1924</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First_MacDonald_ministry" title="First MacDonald ministry">First MacDonald ministry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ramsay_MacDonald_ggbain.29588.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Ramsay_MacDonald_ggbain.29588.jpg/170px-Ramsay_MacDonald_ggbain.29588.jpg" width="170" height="236" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Ramsay_MacDonald_ggbain.29588.jpg/255px-Ramsay_MacDonald_ggbain.29588.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Ramsay_MacDonald_ggbain.29588.jpg/340px-Ramsay_MacDonald_ggbain.29588.jpg 2x" data-file-width="406" data-file-height="564" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ramsay_MacDonald_ggbain.29588.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ramsay_MacDonald" title="Ramsay MacDonald">Ramsay MacDonald</a>: First Labour Prime Minister, 1924 and 1929–31</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1923" title="United Kingdom general election, 1923">1923 general election</a> was fought on the Conservatives' <a href="/wiki/Protectionist" class="mw-redirect" title="Protectionist">protectionist</a> proposals but, although they got the most votes and remained the largest party, they lost their majority in parliament, necessitating the formation of a government supporting <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a>. Thus, with the acquiescence of Asquith's Liberals, <a href="/wiki/Ramsay_MacDonald" title="Ramsay MacDonald">Ramsay MacDonald</a> became the first ever Labour Prime Minister in January 1924, forming the first Labour government, despite Labour only having 191 MPs (less than a third of the House of Commons).
</p><p>Because the government had to rely on the support of the Liberals it was unable to pass any radical legislation. The most significant achievement was the <a href="/wiki/Wheatley_Housing_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Wheatley Housing Act">Wheatley Housing Act</a>, which began a building programme of 500,000 homes for rental to low paid workers. Legislation on education, unemployment, social insurance and tenant protection was also passed. Although no major changes were introduced, the main achievement of the government was to demonstrate that Labour were capable of governing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThorpe200151-53_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThorpe200151-53-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While there were no major labour strikes during his term, MacDonald acted swiftly to end those that did erupt. When the Labour Party executive criticised the government, he replied that, "public doles, <a href="/wiki/Poplar_Rates_Rebellion" title="Poplar Rates Rebellion">Poplarism</a> [local defiance of the national government], strikes for increased wages, limitation of output, not only are not Socialism, but may mislead the spirit and policy of the Socialist movement."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor1965213–214_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor1965213–214-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The government collapsed after only nine months when the Liberals voted for a Select Committee inquiry into the <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Hastings#Campbell_Case" title="Patrick Hastings">Campbell Case</a>, a vote which MacDonald had declared to be a vote of confidence. The ensuing <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1924" title="United Kingdom general election, 1924">1924 general election</a> saw the publication, four days before polling day, of the forged <a href="/wiki/Zinoviev_letter" title="Zinoviev letter">Zinoviev letter</a>, in which Moscow talked about a Communist revolution in Britain. The letter had little impact on the Labour vote—which held up. It was the collapse of the Liberal party that led to the Conservative landslide. The Conservatives were returned to power although Labour increased its vote from 30.7% to a third of the popular vote, most Conservative gains being at the expense of the Liberals. However many Labourites for years blamed their defeat on foul play (the Zinoviev letter), thereby according to <a href="/wiki/A._J._P._Taylor" title="A. J. P. Taylor">A. J. P. Taylor</a> misunderstanding the political forces at work and delaying needed reforms in the party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor1965219–220,_226–227_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor1965219–220,_226–227-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Charles_Loch_Mowat_1955_188–94_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Charles_Loch_Mowat_1955_188–94-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In opposition MacDonald continued his policy of presenting the Labour Party as a moderate force. During the <a href="/wiki/1926_United_Kingdom_general_strike" title="1926 United Kingdom general strike">General Strike of 1926</a> the party opposed the general strike, arguing that the best way to achieve social reforms was through the ballot box. The leaders were also fearful of Communist influence orchestrated from Moscow.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPugh2011_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPugh2011-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Party had a distinctive and suspicious foreign policy based on pacifism. Its leaders believed that peace was impossible because of capitalism, secret diplomacy, and the trade in armaments. That is it stressed material factors that ignored the psychological memories of the Great War, and the highly emotional tensions regarding nationalism and the boundaries of the countries.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Second_Labour_government.2C_1929.E2.80.931931"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_Labour_government,_1929–1931">Second Labour government, 1929–1931</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Second_MacDonald_ministry" title="Second MacDonald ministry">Second MacDonald ministry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oldlabour2.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Oldlabour2.png/170px-Oldlabour2.png" width="170" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/61/Oldlabour2.png 1.5x" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="243" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oldlabour2.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The original "Liberty" logo, in use until 1983</div></div></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1929" title="United Kingdom general election, 1929">1929 general election</a>, the Labour Party became the largest in the House of Commons for the first time, with 287 seats and 37.1% of the popular vote. However MacDonald was still reliant on Liberal support to form a minority government. MacDonald went on to appoint Britain's first woman cabinet minister; <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Bondfield" title="Margaret Bondfield">Margaret Bondfield</a>, who was appointed <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Employment" title="Secretary of State for Employment">Minister of Labour</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>MacDonald's second government was in a stronger parliamentary position than his first, and in 1930 Labour were able to pass legislation to raise unemployment pay, improve wages and conditions in the coal industry (i.e. the issues behind the General Strike) and pass a housing act which focused on slum clearances.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The government, however, soon found itself engulfed in crisis: the <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Crash_of_1929" title="Wall Street Crash of 1929">Wall Street Crash of 1929</a> and eventual <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Great Depression in the United Kingdom">Great Depression</a> occurred soon after the government came to power, and the slump in global trade hit Britain hard. By the end of 1930 unemployment had doubled to over two and a half million.<sup id="cite_ref-To_Build_A_New_Jerusalem_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-To_Build_A_New_Jerusalem-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>  The government had no effective answers to the deteriorating financial situation, and by 1931 there was much fear that the budget was unbalanced, which was born out by the independent <a href="/wiki/May_Report" title="May Report">May Report</a> which triggered a confidence crisis and a run on the pound. The cabinet deadlocked over its response, with several influential members unwilling to support the budget cuts (in particular a cut in the rate of unemployment benefit) which were pressed by the civil service and opposition parties. <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> <a href="/wiki/Philip_Snowden" class="mw-redirect" title="Philip Snowden">Philip Snowden</a> refused to consider <a href="/wiki/Deficit_spending" title="Deficit spending">deficit spending</a> or tariffs as alternative solutions. When a final vote was taken, the Cabinet was split 11-9 with a minority, including many political heavyweights such as <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Henderson" title="Arthur Henderson">Arthur Henderson</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Lansbury" title="George Lansbury">George Lansbury</a>, threatening to resign rather than agree to the cuts. The unworkable split, on 24 August 1931, made the government resign. MacDonald was encouraged by <a href="/wiki/George_V_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="George V of the United Kingdom">King George V</a> to form an all-party <a href="/wiki/National_Government_(United_Kingdom)" title="National Government (United Kingdom)">National Government</a> to deal with the immediate crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The financial crisis grew worse and decisive government action was needed as the leaders of both the Conservative and Liberal Parties met with <a href="/wiki/George_V_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="George V of the United Kingdom">King George V</a>, and MacDonald, at first to discuss support for the spending cuts but later to discuss the shape of the next government.  The king played the central role in demanding a National government be formed. On 24 August, MacDonald agreed to form a National Government composed of men from all parties with the specific aim of balancing the Budget and restoring confidence. The new cabinet had four Labourites (who formed a  "<a href="/wiki/National_Labour_Organisation" title="National Labour Organisation">National Labour</a>" group) who stood  with MacDonald, plus four Conservatives (led by Baldwin, Chamberlain) and two Liberals.  MacDonald's moves aroused great anger among a large majority of Labour Party activists who felt betrayed. Labour unions were strongly opposed and the Labour Party officially repudiated the new National government. It expelled MacDonald and his supporters and made Henderson the leader of the main Labour party. Henderson led it into <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1931" title="United Kingdom general election, 1931">the general election</a> on 27 October against the three-party National coalition. It was a disaster for Labour, which was reduced to a small minority of 52 seats. The Conservative dominated National Government, led by MacDonald won the largest landslide in British political history.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1931 Labour campaigned on opposition to public spending cuts, but found it difficult to defend the record of the party's former government and the fact that most of the cuts had been agreed before it fell. Historian Andrew Thorpe argues that Labour lost credibility by 1931 as unemployment soared, especially in coal, textiles, shipbuilding,  and steel. The working class increasingly lost confidence in the ability of Labour to solve the most pressing problem.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThorpe1996_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThorpe1996-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 2.5 million Irish Catholics in England and Scotland were a major factor in the Labour base in many industrial areas. The Catholic Church had previously tolerated the Labour Party, and denied that it represented true socialism. However, the bishops by 1930 had grown increasingly alarmed at Labour's policies toward Communist Russia, toward birth control and especially toward funding Catholic schools. They warned its members. The Catholic shift against Labour and in favour of the National government played a major role in Labour's losses.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiddell1997_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiddell1997-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1930s_opposition">1930s opposition</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Arthur_Henderson" title="Arthur Henderson">Arthur Henderson</a>, elected in 1931 to succeed MacDonald, lost his seat in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1931" title="United Kingdom general election, 1931">1931 general election</a>. The only former Labour cabinet member who had retained his seat, the pacifist <a href="/wiki/George_Lansbury" title="George Lansbury">George Lansbury</a>, accordingly became party leader.
</p><p>The party experienced another split in 1932 when the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Labour_Party" title="Independent Labour Party">Independent Labour Party</a>, which for some years had been increasingly at odds with the Labour leadership, opted to disaffiliate from the Labour Party and embarked on a long, drawn-out decline.
</p><p>Lansbury resigned as leader in 1935 after public disagreements over foreign policy. He was promptly replaced as leader by his deputy, <a href="/wiki/Clement_Attlee" title="Clement Attlee">Clement Attlee</a>, who would lead the party for two decades. The party experienced a revival in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1935" title="United Kingdom general election, 1935">1935 general election</a>, winning 154 seats and 38% of the popular vote, the highest that Labour had achieved.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As the threat from <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> increased, in the late 1930s the Labour Party gradually abandoned its pacifist stance and came to support re-armament, largely due to the efforts of <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Bevin" title="Ernest Bevin">Ernest Bevin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Dalton" title="Hugh Dalton">Hugh Dalton</a> who by 1937 had also persuaded the party to oppose <a href="/wiki/Neville_Chamberlain" title="Neville Chamberlain">Neville Chamberlain</a>'s policy of <a href="/wiki/Appeasement" title="Appeasement">appeasement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-To_Build_A_New_Jerusalem_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-To_Build_A_New_Jerusalem-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Wartime_coalition.2C_1940.E2.80.931945"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Wartime_coalition,_1940–1945">Wartime coalition, 1940–1945</span></h3>
<p>The party returned to government in 1940 as part of the <a href="/wiki/Churchill_war_ministry" title="Churchill war ministry">wartime coalition</a>. When <a href="/wiki/Neville_Chamberlain" title="Neville Chamberlain">Neville Chamberlain</a> resigned in the spring of 1940, incoming <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> decided to bring the other main parties into a coalition similar to that of the First World War. Clement Attlee was appointed <a href="/wiki/Lord_Privy_Seal" title="Lord Privy Seal">Lord Privy Seal</a> and a member of the war cabinet, eventually becoming the United Kingdom's first <a href="/wiki/Deputy_Prime_Minister" class="mw-redirect" title="Deputy Prime Minister">Deputy Prime Minister</a>.
</p><p>A number of other senior Labour figures also took up senior positions: the trade union leader <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Bevin" title="Ernest Bevin">Ernest Bevin</a>, as <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Employment" title="Secretary of State for Employment">Minister of Labour</a>, directed Britain's wartime economy and allocation of manpower, the veteran Labour statesman <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Morrison" title="Herbert Morrison">Herbert Morrison</a> became <a href="/wiki/Home_Secretary" title="Home Secretary">Home Secretary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Dalton" title="Hugh Dalton">Hugh Dalton</a> was <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_Economic_Warfare" title="Minister of Economic Warfare">Minister of Economic Warfare</a> and later <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Board_of_Trade" title="President of the Board of Trade">President of the Board of Trade</a>, while <a href="/wiki/A._V._Alexander,_1st_Earl_Alexander_of_Hillsborough" title="A. V. Alexander, 1st Earl Alexander of Hillsborough">A. V. Alexander</a> resumed the role he had held in the previous Labour Government as <a href="/wiki/First_Lord_of_the_Admiralty" title="First Lord of the Admiralty">First Lord of the Admiralty</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Attlee_government.2C_1945.E2.80.931951"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Attlee_government,_1945–1951">Attlee government, 1945–1951</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Attlee_ministry" title="Attlee ministry">Attlee ministry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clement_Attlee.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Clement_Attlee.png/170px-Clement_Attlee.png" width="170" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Clement_Attlee.png/255px-Clement_Attlee.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Clement_Attlee.png/340px-Clement_Attlee.png 2x" data-file-width="613" data-file-height="804" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clement_Attlee.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Clement_Attlee" title="Clement Attlee">Clement Attlee</a>: Labour Prime Minister, 1945–51</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aneurin_Bevan_and_his_wife_Jenny_Lee_in_Corwen_(15368872658).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Aneurin_Bevan_and_his_wife_Jenny_Lee_in_Corwen_%2815368872658%29.jpg/170px-Aneurin_Bevan_and_his_wife_Jenny_Lee_in_Corwen_%2815368872658%29.jpg" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Aneurin_Bevan_and_his_wife_Jenny_Lee_in_Corwen_%2815368872658%29.jpg/255px-Aneurin_Bevan_and_his_wife_Jenny_Lee_in_Corwen_%2815368872658%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Aneurin_Bevan_and_his_wife_Jenny_Lee_in_Corwen_%2815368872658%29.jpg/340px-Aneurin_Bevan_and_his_wife_Jenny_Lee_in_Corwen_%2815368872658%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1753" data-file-height="2329" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aneurin_Bevan_and_his_wife_Jenny_Lee_in_Corwen_(15368872658).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aneurin Bevan speaking in October 1952</div></div></div>
<p>At the end of the war in Europe, in May 1945, Labour resolved not to repeat the Liberals' error of 1918, and promptly withdrew from government, on trade union insistence, to contest the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1945" title="United Kingdom general election, 1945">1945 general election</a> in opposition to Churchill's Conservatives. Surprising many observers,<sup id="cite_ref-BBCreport_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCreport-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Labour won a formidable victory, winning just under 50% of the vote with a majority of 159 seats.<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although <a href="/wiki/Clement_Attlee" title="Clement Attlee">Clement Attlee</a> was no great radical himself,<sup id="cite_ref-Hall2011_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hall2011-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Attlee's government proved one of the most radical British governments of the 20th century, enacting <a href="/wiki/Keynesian" class="mw-redirect" title="Keynesian">Keynesian</a> economic policies, presiding over a policy of nationalising major industries and utilities including the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a>, coal mining, the steel industry, electricity, gas, and inland transport (including railways, road haulage and canals). It developed and implemented the "cradle to grave" <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">welfare state</a> conceived by the economist <a href="/wiki/William_Beveridge" title="William Beveridge">William Beveridge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Marsh2007_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marsh2007-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Justin2013_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Justin2013-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jago2014_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jago2014-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> To this day, most people in the United Kingdom see the 1948 creation of Britain's <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">National Health Service</a> (NHS) under health minister <a href="/wiki/Aneurin_Bevan" title="Aneurin Bevan">Aneurin Bevan</a>, which gave publicly funded medical treatment for all, as Labour's proudest achievement.<sup id="cite_ref-Pearce2006_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pearce2006-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Attlee's government also began the process of dismantling the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> when it granted independence to India and Pakistan in 1947, followed by Burma (Myanmar) and Ceylon (Sri Lanka) the following year. At a secret meeting in January 1947, Attlee and six cabinet ministers, including Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Bevin" title="Ernest Bevin">Ernest Bevin</a>, decided to proceed with the development of Britain's <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom" title="Nuclear weapons and the United Kingdom">nuclear weapons programme</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-To_Build_A_New_Jerusalem_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-To_Build_A_New_Jerusalem-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> in opposition to the pacifist and anti-nuclear stances of a large element inside the Labour Party.
</p><p>Labour went on to win the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1950" title="United Kingdom general election, 1950">1950 general election</a>, but with a much reduced majority of five seats. Soon afterwards, defence became a divisive issue within the party, especially defence spending (which reached a peak of 14% of GDP in 1951 during the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-Clark,_Sir_George_1987_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark,_Sir_George_1987-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> straining public finances and forcing savings elsewhere. The Chancellor of the Exchequer, <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Gaitskell" title="Hugh Gaitskell">Hugh Gaitskell</a>, introduced charges for NHS dentures and spectacles, causing Bevan, along with <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a> (then President of the Board of Trade), to resign over the dilution of the principle of free treatment on which the NHS had been established.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1951" title="United Kingdom general election, 1951">1951 general election</a>, Labour narrowly lost to Churchill's Conservatives, despite receiving the larger share of the popular vote – its highest ever vote numerically. Most of the changes introduced by the 1945–51 Labour government were accepted by the Conservatives and became part of the "<a href="/wiki/Post-war_consensus" title="Post-war consensus">post-war consensus</a>" that lasted until the late 1970s. Food and clothing rationing, however, still in place since the war, were swiftly relaxed, then abandoned from about 1953.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Post-war_consensus.2C_1951.E2.80.931964"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war_consensus,_1951–1964">Post-war consensus, 1951–1964</span></h3>
<p>Following the defeat of 1951 the party spent 13 years in opposition. The party suffered an ideological split, between the party's left-wing followers of <a href="/wiki/Aneurin_Bevan" title="Aneurin Bevan">Aneurin Bevan</a> (known as <a href="/wiki/Bevanites" class="mw-redirect" title="Bevanites">Bevanites</a>), and the right-wing of the party following <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Gaitskell" title="Hugh Gaitskell">Hugh Gaitskell</a> (known as <a href="/wiki/Gaitskellites" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaitskellites">Gaitskellites</a>) while the postwar economic recovery and the social effects of Attlee's reforms made the public broadly content with the Conservative governments of the time. The ageing Attlee contested his final general election in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1955" title="United Kingdom general election, 1955">1955</a>, which saw Labour lose ground, and he retired shortly after.
</p><p>His replacement, Hugh Gaitskell, associated with the right wing of the party, struggled in dealing with internal party divisions in the late 1950s and early 1960s (particularly over <a href="/wiki/Clause_IV" title="Clause IV">Clause IV</a> of the Labour Party Constitution, which was viewed as Labour's commitment to <a href="/wiki/Nationalisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Nationalisation">nationalisation</a> and Gaitskell wanted scrapped<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarlow2008224Beech2006218Clark201266HeathJowellCurtice2001106Heppell201238Jones19968KennySmith2013110Leach2015118_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarlow2008224Beech2006218Clark201266HeathJowellCurtice2001106Heppell201238Jones19968KennySmith2013110Leach2015118-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clause_IV_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clause_IV-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>). Under Gaitskell, Labour lost their third general election in a row in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1959" title="United Kingdom general election, 1959">1959</a>. In 1963, Gaitskell's sudden death from a rare illness made way for <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a> to lead the party.
</p>
<h3><span id="Wilson_government.2C_1964.E2.80.931970"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Wilson_government,_1964–1970">Wilson government, 1964–1970</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First_Wilson_ministry" class="mw-redirect" title="First Wilson ministry">First Wilson ministry</a></div>
<p>A downturn in the economy and a series of scandals in the early 1960s (the most notorious being the <a href="/wiki/Profumo_affair" title="Profumo affair">Profumo affair</a>) had engulfed the Conservative government by 1963. The Labour Party returned to government with a 4-seat majority under Wilson in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1964" title="United Kingdom general election, 1964">1964 general election</a> but increased its majority to 96 in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1966" title="United Kingdom general election, 1966">1966 general election</a>.
</p><p>Wilson's government was responsible for a number of sweeping social and educational reforms under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Home_Secretary" title="Home Secretary">Home Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Roy_Jenkins" title="Roy Jenkins">Roy Jenkins</a> such as the abolishment of the <a href="/wiki/Death_penalty" class="mw-redirect" title="Death penalty">death penalty</a> in 1964, the legalisation of <a href="/wiki/Abortion" title="Abortion">abortion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Homosexuality" title="Homosexuality">homosexuality</a> (initially only for men aged 21 or over, and only in <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales" title="England and Wales">England and Wales</a>) in 1967 and the abolition of theatre censorship in 1968. <a href="/wiki/Comprehensive_education" class="mw-redirect" title="Comprehensive education">Comprehensive education</a> was expanded and the <a href="/wiki/Open_University" title="Open University">Open University</a> created. However Wilson's government had inherited a large trade deficit that led to a currency crisis and ultimately a doomed attempt to stave off devaluation of the pound. Labour went on to lose the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1970" title="United Kingdom general election, 1970">1970 general election</a> to the Conservatives under <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Spell_in_opposition.2C_1970.E2.80.931974"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Spell_in_opposition,_1970–1974">Spell in opposition, 1970–1974</span></h3>
<p>After losing the 1970 general election, Labour returned to opposition, but retained Harold Wilson as Leader. Heath's government soon ran into trouble over <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> and a dispute with miners in 1973 which led to the "<a href="/wiki/Three-day_week" class="mw-redirect" title="Three-day week">three-day week</a>". The 1970s proved a difficult time to be in government for both the Conservatives and Labour due to the <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">1973 oil crisis</a> which caused high inflation and a global recession.
</p><p>The Labour Party was returned to power again under Wilson a few weeks after the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_February_1974" title="United Kingdom general election, February 1974">February 1974 general election</a>, forming a minority government with the support of the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Unionist" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulster Unionist">Ulster Unionists</a>. The Conservatives were unable to form a government alone as they had fewer seats despite receiving more votes numerically. It was the first general election since 1924 in which both main parties had received less than 40% of the popular vote and the first of six successive general elections in which Labour failed to reach 40% of the popular vote. In a bid to gain a majority, a second election was soon called for <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_October_1974" title="United Kingdom general election, October 1974">October 1974</a> in which Labour, still with Harold Wilson as leader, won a slim majority of three, gaining just 18 seats taking its total to 319.
</p>
<h3><span id="Majority_to_minority.2C_1974.E2.80.931979"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Majority_to_minority,_1974–1979">Majority to minority, 1974–1979</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Labour_Government_1974%E2%80%9379" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour Government 1974–79">Labour Government 1974–79</a></div>
<p>For much of its time in office the Labour government struggled with serious economic problems and a precarious majority in the Commons, while the party's internal dissent over Britain's membership of the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a>, which Britain had entered under Edward Heath in 1972, led in 1975 to a <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_European_Communities_membership_referendum,_1975" title="United Kingdom European Communities membership referendum, 1975">national referendum</a> on the issue in which two thirds of the public supported continued membership.
</p><p>Harold Wilson's personal popularity remained reasonably high but he unexpectedly resigned as Prime Minister in 1976 citing health reasons, and was replaced by <a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a>. The Wilson and Callaghan governments of the 1970s tried to control inflation (which reached 23.7% in 1975<sup id="cite_ref-SeldonHickson2004_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SeldonHickson2004-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>) by a policy of <a href="/wiki/Incomes_policy" title="Incomes policy">wage restraint</a>. This was fairly successful, reducing inflation to 7.4% by 1978.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThorpe2001_17-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThorpe2001-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SeldonHickson2004_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SeldonHickson2004-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> However it led to increasingly strained relations between the government and the trade unions.
</p><p>Fear of advances by the nationalist parties, particularly in Scotland, led to the suppression of a <a href="/wiki/McCrone_report" title="McCrone report">report from Scottish Office economist Gavin McCrone</a> that suggested that an independent Scotland would be "chronically in surplus".<sup id="cite_ref-SNP_Youth_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SNP_Youth-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> By 1977 by-election losses and defections to the breakaway <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Labour_Party_(1976)" title="Scottish Labour Party (1976)">Scottish Labour Party</a> left Callaghan heading a minority government, forced to do deals with smaller parties in order to govern. An arrangement negotiated in 1977 with <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal</a> leader <a href="/wiki/David_Steel" title="David Steel">David Steel</a>, known as the <a href="/wiki/Lib%E2%80%93Lab_pact" title="Lib–Lab pact">Lib–Lab pact</a>, ended after one year. Deals were then forged with various small parties including the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">Scottish National Party</a> (SNP) and the Welsh nationalist <a href="/wiki/Plaid_Cymru" title="Plaid Cymru">Plaid Cymru</a>, prolonging the life of the government.
</p><p>The nationalist parties, in turn, demanded <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolution</a> to their respective constituent countries in return for their supporting the government. When referendums for Scottish and Welsh devolution were held in March 1979 the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_devolution_referendum,_1979" title="Welsh devolution referendum, 1979">Welsh devolution referendum</a> saw a large majority vote against, while the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_devolution_referendum,_1979" title="Scottish devolution referendum, 1979">Scottish referendum</a> returned a narrow majority in favour without reaching the required threshold of 40% support. When the Labour government duly refused to push ahead with setting up the proposed Scottish Assembly, the SNP withdrew its support for the government: this finally brought the government down as the Conservatives triggered a <a href="/wiki/1979_vote_of_no_confidence_in_the_government_of_James_Callaghan" class="mw-redirect" title="1979 vote of no confidence in the government of James Callaghan">vote of confidence</a> in Callaghan's government that was lost by a single vote on 28 March 1979, necessitating a general election.
</p><p>By 1978 the economy had started to show signs of recovery, with inflation falling to single digits, unemployment falling, and living standards starting to rise during the year.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Labour's opinion poll ratings also improved, with most showing the party to have gained a narrow lead.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThorpe2001_17-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThorpe2001-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Callaghan had been widely expected to call a general election in the autumn of 1978 to take advantage of the improved situation. In the event he decided to extend his wage restraint policy for another year hoping that the economy would be in a better shape for a 1979 election. However, during the winter of 1978–79 there were widespread strikes among lorry drivers, railway workers, car workers and local government and hospital workers in favour of higher pay-rises that caused significant disruption to everyday life. These events came to be dubbed the "<a href="/wiki/Winter_of_Discontent" title="Winter of Discontent">Winter of Discontent</a>".
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1979" title="United Kingdom general election, 1979">1979 general election</a> Labour was heavily defeated by the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservatives</a> now led by <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a>. The Labour vote held up in the election, with the party receiving a similar number of votes as in 1974. However the Conservative Party achieved big increases in support in the Midlands and South of England, benefiting from both a surge in turnout and votes lost by the ailing Liberals.
</p>
<h3><span id="Internal_conflict_and_opposition.2C_1979.E2.80.931997"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_conflict_and_opposition,_1979–1997">Internal conflict and opposition, 1979–1997</span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Labour_Party_(UK)&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <small><i>(February 2017)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>After its defeat in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1979" title="United Kingdom general election, 1979">1979 general election</a> the Labour Party underwent a period of internal rivalry between the left represented by <a href="/wiki/Tony_Benn" title="Tony Benn">Tony Benn</a>, and the right represented by <a href="/wiki/Denis_Healey" title="Denis Healey">Denis Healey</a>. The election of <a href="/wiki/Michael_Foot" title="Michael Foot">Michael Foot</a> as leader in 1980, and the leftist policies he espoused, such as <a href="/wiki/Unilateral_nuclear_disarmament" class="mw-redirect" title="Unilateral nuclear disarmament">unilateral nuclear disarmament</a>, leaving the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a> and <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>, closer governmental influence in the banking system, the creation of a <a href="/wiki/National_minimum_wage" class="mw-redirect" title="National minimum wage">national minimum wage</a> and a ban on <a href="/wiki/Fox_hunting" title="Fox hunting">fox hunting</a><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> led in 1981 to four former cabinet ministers from the right of the Labour Party (<a href="/wiki/Shirley_Williams" title="Shirley Williams">Shirley Williams</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Rodgers" class="mw-redirect" title="William Rodgers">William Rodgers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roy_Jenkins" title="Roy Jenkins">Roy Jenkins</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Owen" title="David Owen">David Owen</a>) forming the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_(UK)" title="Social Democratic Party (UK)">Social Democratic Party</a>. Benn was only narrowly defeated by Healey in a bitterly fought deputy leadership election in 1981 after the introduction of an electoral college intended to widen the voting franchise to elect the leader and their deputy. By 1982, the <a href="/wiki/National_Executive_Committee" title="National Executive Committee">National Executive Committee</a> had concluded that the <a href="/wiki/Entryism" title="Entryism">entryist</a> <a href="/wiki/Militant_tendency" class="mw-redirect" title="Militant tendency">Militant tendency</a> group were in contravention of the party's constitution. The <i>Militant</i> newspaper's five member editorial board were expelled on 22 February 1983.
</p><p>The Labour Party was defeated heavily in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1983" title="United Kingdom general election, 1983">1983 general election</a>, winning only 27.6% of the vote, its lowest share since <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1918" title="United Kingdom general election, 1918">1918</a>, and receiving only half a million votes more than the <a href="/wiki/SDP-Liberal_Alliance" class="mw-redirect" title="SDP-Liberal Alliance">SDP-Liberal Alliance</a> who leader Michael Foot condemned for "siphoning" Labour support and enabling the Conservatives to greatly increase their majority of parliamentary seats.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Neil_Kinnock_(1989).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Neil_Kinnock_%281989%29.jpg/170px-Neil_Kinnock_%281989%29.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Neil_Kinnock_%281989%29.jpg/255px-Neil_Kinnock_%281989%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Neil_Kinnock_%281989%29.jpg/340px-Neil_Kinnock_%281989%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="557" data-file-height="743" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Neil_Kinnock_(1989).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Neil_Kinnock" title="Neil Kinnock">Neil Kinnock</a>: leader of the party in opposition, 1983–92</div></div></div>
<p>Foot resigned and was replaced as leader by <a href="/wiki/Neil_Kinnock" title="Neil Kinnock">Neil Kinnock</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Roy_Hattersley" title="Roy Hattersley">Roy Hattersley</a> as his deputy. The new leadership progressively dropped unpopular policies. The <a href="/wiki/UK_miners%27_strike_(1984%E2%80%9385)" title="UK miners&#39; strike (1984–85)">miners strike of 1984–85</a> over coal mine closures, which divided the NUM as well as the Labour Party, and the <a href="/wiki/Wapping_dispute" title="Wapping dispute">Wapping dispute</a> led to clashes with the left of the party, and negative coverage in most of the press. Tabloid vilification of the so-called <a href="/wiki/Loony_left" title="Loony left">loony left</a> continued to taint the parliamentary party by association from the activities of "extra-parliamentary" militants in local government.
</p><p>The alliances which campaigns such as <a href="/wiki/Lesbians_and_Gays_Support_the_Miners" title="Lesbians and Gays Support the Miners">Lesbians and Gays Support the Miners</a> forged between <a href="/wiki/LGBT" title="LGBT">lesbian, gay, bisexual, and transgender</a> (LGBT) and <a href="/wiki/Labour_movement" title="Labour movement">labour groups</a>, as well as the Labour Party itself, also proved to be an important turning point in the progression of LGBT issues in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelliher2014_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelliher2014-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> At the 1985 Labour Party conference in Bournemouth, a resolution committing the party to support LGBT equality rights passed for the first time<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelliher2014256_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelliher2014256-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> due to block voting support from the <a href="/wiki/National_Union_of_Mineworkers_(Great_Britain)" title="National Union of Mineworkers (Great Britain)">National Union of Mineworkers</a>.
</p><p>Labour improved its performance in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1987" title="United Kingdom general election, 1987">1987</a>, gaining 20 seats and so reducing the Conservative majority from 143 to 102. They were now firmly re-established as the second political party in Britain as the Alliance had once again failed to make a breakthrough with seats. A merger of the SDP and Liberals formed the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a>. Following the 1987 election, the National Executive Committee resumed disciplinary action against members of Militant, who remained in the party, leading to further expulsions of their activists and the two MPs who supported the group. During the 1980s radically socialist members of the party were often described as the "<a href="/wiki/Loony_left" title="Loony left">loony left</a>", particularly in the <a href="/wiki/Print_media" class="mw-redirect" title="Print media">print media</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Julian_Petley_2005_85–107_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Julian_Petley_2005_85–107-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The print media in the 1980s also began using the pejorative "hard left" to sometimes describe <a href="/wiki/Trotskyism" title="Trotskyism">Trotskyist</a> groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Militant_tendency" class="mw-redirect" title="Militant tendency">Militant tendency</a>, <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Organiser" title="Socialist Organiser">Socialist Organiser</a> and <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Action_(UK)" title="Socialist Action (UK)">Socialist Action</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShaw1988267_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShaw1988267-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1988, <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_1988" title="Labour Party (UK) leadership election, 1988">Kinnock was challenged</a> by <a href="/wiki/Tony_Benn" title="Tony Benn">Tony Benn</a> for the party leadership. Based on the percentages, 183 members of parliament supported Kinnock, while Benn was backed by 37. With a clear majority, Kinnock remained leader of the Labour Party.<sup id="cite_ref-kinnockchallenge_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kinnockchallenge-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 1990 following a contested leadership election, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> resigned as leader of the Conservative Party and was succeeded as leader and Prime Minister by <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a>. Most opinion polls had shown Labour comfortably ahead of the Tories for more than a year before Thatcher's resignation, with the fall in Tory support blamed largely on her introduction of the unpopular <a href="/wiki/Tax_per_head" class="mw-redirect" title="Tax per head">poll tax</a>, combined with the fact that the economy was <a href="/wiki/Early_1990s_recession" title="Early 1990s recession">sliding into recession</a> at the time.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Old_Logo_Labour_Party.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4c/Old_Logo_Labour_Party.svg/170px-Old_Logo_Labour_Party.svg.png" width="170" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4c/Old_Logo_Labour_Party.svg/255px-Old_Logo_Labour_Party.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4c/Old_Logo_Labour_Party.svg/340px-Old_Logo_Labour_Party.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="329" data-file-height="304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Old_Logo_Labour_Party.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Labour Party logo under Kinnock, Smith and Blair's leaderships</div></div></div>
<p>The change of leader in the Tory government saw a turnaround in support for the Tories, who regularly topped the opinion polls throughout 1991 although Labour regained the lead more than once.
</p><p>The "yo-yo" in the opinion polls continued into 1992, though after November 1990 any Labour lead in the polls was rarely sufficient for a majority. Major resisted Kinnock's calls for a general election throughout 1991. Kinnock campaigned on the theme "It's Time for a Change", urging voters to elect a new government after more than a decade of unbroken Conservative rule. However, the Conservatives themselves had undergone a change of leader from Thatcher to Major, and replaced the Community Charge.  From the outset, it was clearly a well-received change, as Labour's 14-point lead in the November 1990 "Poll of Polls" was replaced by an 8% Tory lead a month later.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1992" title="United Kingdom general election, 1992">1992 general election</a> was widely tipped to result in a hung parliament or a narrow Labour majority, but in the event the Conservatives were returned to power, though with a much reduced majority of 21.<sup id="cite_ref-1992:_Tories_win_again_against_odds_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1992:_Tories_win_again_against_odds-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Despite the increased number of seats and votes, it was still an incredibly disappointing result for supporters of the Labour party. For the first time in over 30 years there was serious doubt among the public and the media as to whether Labour could ever return to government.
</p><p>Kinnock then resigned as leader and was replaced by <a href="/wiki/John_Smith_(Labour_Party_leader)" title="John Smith (Labour Party leader)">John Smith</a>. Once again the battle erupted between the old guard on the party's left and those identified as "modernisers".  The old guard argued that trends showed they were regaining strength under Smith's strong leadership.  Meanwhile, the breakaway SDP merged with the Liberal Party. The new <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a> seemed to pose a major threat to the Labour base. <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> (the Shadow Home Secretary) had an entirely different vision. Blair, the leader of the "modernising" faction (<a href="/wiki/Blairites" class="mw-redirect" title="Blairites">Blairites</a>), argued that the long-term trends had to be reversed, arguing that the party was too locked into a base that was shrinking, since it was based on the working-class, on trade unions, and on residents of subsidised council housing. Blairites argued that the rapidly growing middle class was largely ignored, as well as more ambitious working-class families. It was said that they aspired to become middle-class, but accepted the Conservative argument that Labour was holding ambitious people back, with its levelling down policies.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (June 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> They increasingly saw Labour in a negative light, regarding  higher taxes and higher interest rates. To present a fresh face and new policies to the electorate, <a href="/wiki/New_Labour" title="New Labour">New Labour</a> needed more than fresh leaders; it had to jettison outdated policies, argued the modernisers.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The first step was procedural, but essential. Calling on the slogan, "<a href="/wiki/One_Member,_One_Vote" class="mw-redirect" title="One Member, One Vote">One Member, One Vote</a>" Blair (with some help from Smith) defeated the union element and ended <a href="/wiki/Voting_bloc" title="Voting bloc">block voting</a> by leaders of <a href="/wiki/Labour_unions" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour unions">labour unions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERentoul2001206–218_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERentoul2001206–218-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Blair and the modernisers called for radical adjustment of Party goals by repealing "Clause IV", the historic commitment to nationalisation of industry. This was achieved in 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERentoul2001249–266_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERentoul2001249–266-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Black_Wednesday" title="Black Wednesday">Black Wednesday</a> in September 1992 damaged  the Conservative government's reputation for economic competence, and by the end of that year Labour had a comfortable lead over the Tories in the opinion polls. Although the recession was declared over in April 1993 and a period of strong and sustained economic growth followed, coupled with a relatively swift fall in unemployment, the Labour lead in the opinion polls remained strong. However, Smith died from a heart attack in May 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="New_Labour.2C_1994.E2.80.932010"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Labour,_1994–2010">New Labour, 1994–2010</span></h3>
