<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/New_Labour" title="New Labour">New Labour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Premiership_of_Tony_Blair" title="Premiership of Tony Blair">Premiership of Tony Blair</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Premiership_of_Gordon_Brown" title="Premiership of Gordon Brown">Premiership of Gordon Brown</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:310px;max-width:310px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:TonyBlairBasra.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/TonyBlairBasra.JPG/150px-TonyBlairBasra.JPG" width="150" height="218" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/TonyBlairBasra.JPG/225px-TonyBlairBasra.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/TonyBlairBasra.JPG/300px-TonyBlairBasra.JPG 2x" data-file-width="408" data-file-height="593" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a>: Labour Prime Minister, 1997–2007</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:154px;max-width:154px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:GordonBrown1234_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/GordonBrown1234_cropped.jpg/152px-GordonBrown1234_cropped.jpg" width="152" height="217" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/GordonBrown1234_cropped.jpg/228px-GordonBrown1234_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/GordonBrown1234_cropped.jpg/304px-GordonBrown1234_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="598" data-file-height="854" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a>: Labour Prime Minister, 2007–2010</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> continued to move the party further to the centre, abandoning the largely symbolic <a href="/wiki/Clause_IV" title="Clause IV">Clause Four</a> at the 1995 mini-conference in a strategy to increase the party's appeal to "<a href="/wiki/Middle_England" title="Middle England">middle England</a>". More than a simple re-branding, however, the project would draw upon the <a href="/wiki/Third_Way_(centrism)" class="mw-redirect" title="Third Way (centrism)">Third Way</a> strategy, informed by the thoughts of the British sociologist <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Giddens" title="Anthony Giddens">Anthony Giddens</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/New_Labour" title="New Labour">New Labour</a> was first termed as an alternative branding for the Labour Party, dating from a conference slogan first used by the Labour Party in 1994, which was later seen in a draft manifesto published by the party in 1996, called <i><a href="/wiki/New_Labour,_New_Life_For_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="New Labour, New Life For Britain">New Labour, New Life For Britain</a></i>. It was a continuation of the trend that had begun under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Neil_Kinnock" title="Neil Kinnock">Neil Kinnock</a>. New Labour as a name has no official status, but remains in common use to distinguish modernisers from those holding to more traditional positions, normally referred to as "Old Labour".
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>New Labour is a party of ideas and ideals but not of outdated ideology. What counts is what works. The objectives are radical. The means will be modern.<sup id="cite_ref-Labour-Party.org.uk_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Labour-Party.org.uk-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>The Labour Party won the 1997 general election with a landslide majority of 179; it was the largest Labour majority ever, and at the time the largest swing to a political party achieved since <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1945" title="United Kingdom general election, 1945">1945</a>. Over the next decade, a wide range of progressive social reforms were enacted,<sup id="cite_ref-Nigel_has_written_a_key_list_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nigel_has_written_a_key_list-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reforms_–_ISSA_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reforms_–_ISSA-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> with millions lifted out of poverty during Labour's time in office largely as a result of various tax and benefit reforms.<sup id="cite_ref-dwp.gov.uk_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dwp.gov.uk-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Poverty.org.uk_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poverty.org.uk-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-oecd.org_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oecd.org-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Among the early acts of Blair's government were the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/National_minimum_wage" class="mw-redirect" title="National minimum wage">national minimum wage</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolution</a> of power to Scotland, Wales and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>, major changes to the regulation of the banking system, and the re-creation of a citywide government body for London, the <a href="/wiki/Greater_London_Authority" title="Greater London Authority">Greater London Authority</a>, with its own elected-<a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_London" title="Mayor of London">Mayor</a>.
</p><p>Combined with a Conservative opposition that had yet to organise effectively under <a href="/wiki/William_Hague" title="William Hague">William Hague</a>, and the continuing popularity of Blair, Labour went on to win the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2001" title="United Kingdom general election, 2001">2001 election</a> with a similar majority, dubbed the "quiet landslide" by the media.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Daily_Telegraph_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Daily_Telegraph-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> In 2003 Labour introduced <a href="/wiki/Tax_credits" class="mw-redirect" title="Tax credits">tax credits</a>, government  top-ups to the pay of low-wage workers.
</p><p>A perceived turning point was when Blair controversially allied himself with US President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> in supporting the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a>, which caused him to lose much of his political support.<sup id="cite_ref-Deutsche_Welle_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deutsche_Welle-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Kofi_Annan" title="Kofi Annan">UN Secretary-General</a>, among many, considered the war illegal and a violation of the <a href="/wiki/UN_Charter" class="mw-redirect" title="UN Charter">UN Charter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tucker2015_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tucker2015-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcClintock2010150_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcClintock2010150-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The Iraq War was deeply unpopular in most western countries, with Western governments divided in their support<sup id="cite_ref-Bennhold_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bennhold-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> and under pressure from <a href="/wiki/Protests_against_the_Iraq_War" title="Protests against the Iraq War">worldwide popular protests</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The decisions that led up to the Iraq war and its subsequent conduct were the subject of <a href="/wiki/Sir_John_Chilcot" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir John Chilcot">Sir John Chilcot</a>'s <a href="/wiki/The_Iraq_Inquiry" class="mw-redirect" title="The Iraq Inquiry">Iraq Inquiry</a> (commonly referred to as the "Chilcot report").<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2005" title="United Kingdom general election, 2005">2005 general election</a>, Labour was re-elected for a third term, but with a reduced majority of 66 and popular vote of only 35.2%, the lowest percentage of any majority government in British history.
</p><p>Blair announced in September 2006 that he would quit as leader within the year, though he had been under pressure to quit earlier than May 2007 in order to get a new leader in place before the May elections which were expected to be disastrous for Labour.<sup id="cite_ref-I_will_quit_within_a_year_–_Blair_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-I_will_quit_within_a_year_–_Blair-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> In the event, the party did lose power in Scotland to a minority <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">Scottish National Party</a> government at the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament_election,_2007" title="Scottish Parliament election, 2007">2007 elections</a> and, shortly after this, Blair resigned as Prime Minister and was replaced by his <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a>. Although the party experienced a brief rise in the polls after this, its popularity soon slumped to its lowest level since the days of <a href="/wiki/Michael_Foot" title="Michael Foot">Michael Foot</a>. During May 2008, Labour suffered heavy defeats in the <a href="/wiki/London_mayoral_election,_2008" title="London mayoral election, 2008">London mayoral election</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_local_elections,_2008" title="United Kingdom local elections, 2008">local elections</a> and the loss in the <a href="/wiki/Crewe_and_Nantwich_by-election,_2008" title="Crewe and Nantwich by-election, 2008">Crewe and Nantwich by-election</a>, culminating in the party registering its worst ever opinion poll result since records began in 1943, of 23%, with many citing Brown's leadership as a key factor.<sup id="cite_ref-Lovell_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lovell-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Membership of the party also reached a low ebb, falling to 156,205 by the end of 2009: over 40 per cent of the 405,000 peak reached in 1997 and thought to be the lowest total since the party was founded.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-parliament.uk_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parliament.uk-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Finance proved a major problem for the Labour Party during this period; a "<a href="/wiki/Cash_for_peerages" class="mw-redirect" title="Cash for peerages">cash for peerages</a>" scandal under Blair resulted in the drying up of many major sources of donations. Declining party membership, partially due to the reduction of activists' influence upon policy-making under the reforms of <a href="/wiki/Neil_Kinnock" title="Neil Kinnock">Neil Kinnock</a> and Blair, also contributed to financial problems.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Between January and March 2008, the Labour Party received just over £3&#160;million in donations and were £17&#160;million in debt; compared to the Conservatives' £6&#160;million in donations and £12&#160;million in debt.<sup id="cite_ref-electoralcommission.org.uk_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-electoralcommission.org.uk-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> These debts eventually mounted to £24.5 million, and were finally fully repaid in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-independent-20151126_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-independent-20151126-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2010" title="United Kingdom general election, 2010">2010 general election</a> on 6 May that year, Labour with 29.0% of the vote won the second largest number of seats (258). The Conservatives with 36.5% of the vote won the largest number of seats (307), but <a href="/wiki/Hung_parliament" title="Hung parliament">no party had an overall majority</a>, meaning that Labour could still remain in power if they managed to form a coalition with at least one smaller party.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian.co.uk_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian.co.uk-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> However, the Labour Party would have had to form a coalition with more than one other smaller party to gain an overall majority; anything less would result in a minority government.<sup id="cite_ref-Wintour_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wintour-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> On 10 May 2010, after talks to form a coalition with the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a> broke down, Brown announced his intention to stand down as Leader before the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_Conference" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour Party Conference">Labour Party Conference</a> but a day later resigned as both <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister</a> and party leader.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Independent_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Independent-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Opposition.2C_2010.E2.80.932015"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposition,_2010–2015">Opposition, 2010–2015</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ed_Miliband_on_August_27,_2010_cropped-an_less_red-2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Ed_Miliband_on_August_27%2C_2010_cropped-an_less_red-2.jpg/170px-Ed_Miliband_on_August_27%2C_2010_cropped-an_less_red-2.jpg" width="170" height="244" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Ed_Miliband_on_August_27%2C_2010_cropped-an_less_red-2.jpg/255px-Ed_Miliband_on_August_27%2C_2010_cropped-an_less_red-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Ed_Miliband_on_August_27%2C_2010_cropped-an_less_red-2.jpg/340px-Ed_Miliband_on_August_27%2C_2010_cropped-an_less_red-2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="414" data-file-height="594" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ed_Miliband_on_August_27,_2010_cropped-an_less_red-2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ed_Miliband" title="Ed Miliband">Ed Miliband</a>, leader of the party, 2010–2015</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Harriet_Harman" title="Harriet Harman">Harriet Harman</a> became the <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Opposition_(United_Kingdom)" title="Leader of the Opposition (United Kingdom)">Leader of the Opposition</a> and acting Leader of the Labour Party following the resignation of <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a> on 11 May 2010, pending a <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_2010" title="Labour Party (UK) leadership election, 2010">leadership election</a><sup id="cite_ref-Harman_made_acting_Labour_leader_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harman_made_acting_Labour_leader-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> subsequently won by <a href="/wiki/Ed_Miliband" title="Ed Miliband">Ed Miliband</a>. Miliband emphasised "responsible capitalism" and greater state intervention to change the balance of the economy away from financial services.<sup id="cite_ref-Miliband_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miliband-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Tackling vested interests<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> and opening up closed circles in British society<sup id="cite_ref-The_Labour_Party_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Labour_Party-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> were themes he returned to a number of times. Miliband also argued for greater regulation of banks and energy companies.<sup id="cite_ref-New_Statesman_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-New_Statesman-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_Labour_Party" title="Parliamentary Labour Party">Parliamentary Labour Party</a> voted to abolish <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)_Shadow_Cabinet_election,_2010" title="Labour Party (UK) Shadow Cabinet election, 2010">Shadow Cabinet elections</a> in 2011,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> ratified by the National Executive Committee and Party Conference. Henceforth the leader of the party chose the Shadow Cabinet members.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc-20110926_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-20110926-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The party's performance held up in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_local_elections,_2012" title="United Kingdom local elections, 2012">local elections</a> in 2012 with Labour consolidating its position in the North and Midlands, while also regaining some ground in Southern England.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> In Wales the party enjoyed good successes, regaining control of most Welsh councils lost in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_local_elections,_2008" title="United Kingdom local elections, 2008">2008</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Cardiff_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardiff Council">Cardiff</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Scottish_local_elections,_2012" title="Scottish local elections, 2012">Scotland</a>, Labour held overall control of <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_City_Council" title="Glasgow City Council">Glasgow City Council</a> despite some predictions to the contrary,<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> and also enjoyed a +3.26 swing across Scotland. Results in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> were mixed: <a href="/wiki/Ken_Livingstone" title="Ken Livingstone">Ken Livingstone</a> lost the election for <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_London" title="Mayor of London">Mayor of London</a>, but the party gained its highest ever representation in the <a href="/wiki/Greater_London_Authority" title="Greater London Authority">Greater London Authority</a> in the concurrent <a href="/wiki/London_Assembly_election,_2012" title="London Assembly election, 2012">assembly election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 1 March 2014, at a special conference the party reformed internal Labour election procedures, including replacing the electoral college system for selecting new leaders with a "one member, one vote" system following the recommendation of a review by former general-secretary <a href="/wiki/Ray_Collins,_Baron_Collins_of_Highbury" title="Ray Collins, Baron Collins of Highbury">Ray Collins</a>. Mass membership would be encouraged by allowing "registered supporters" to join at a low cost, as well as full membership. Members from the trade unions would also have to explicitly "opt in" rather than "opt out" of paying a political levy to Labour.<sup id="cite_ref-independent-20140228_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-independent-20140228-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-guardian-20140301_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-20140301-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-collins-review_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-collins-review-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The party edged out the Conservatives in the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_2014_(United_Kingdom)" title="European Parliament election, 2014 (United Kingdom)">May 2014 European parliamentary elections</a> winning 20 seats to the Conservatives' 19. However the UK Independence Party won 24 seats.<sup id="cite_ref-UKIP_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UKIP-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Labour also gained 324 councillors in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_local_elections,_2014" title="United Kingdom local elections, 2014">2014 local elections</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Council_Elections_2014_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Council_Elections_2014-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2014, Shadow Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Ed_Balls" title="Ed Balls">Ed Balls</a> outlined his plans to cut the government's <a href="/wiki/Current_account" title="Current account">current account</a> deficit, and the party carried these plans into the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2015" title="United Kingdom general election, 2015">2015 general election</a>. Whereas Conservatives campaigned for a surplus on all government spending, including investment, by 2018–19, Labour stated it would <a href="/wiki/Balanced_budget" title="Balanced budget">balance the budget</a>, excluding investment, by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2015" title="United Kingdom general election, 2015">2015 general election</a> unexpectedly resulted in a net loss of seats, with Labour representation falling to 232 seats in the House of Commons.<sup id="cite_ref-How_many_seats_did_Labour_win_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-How_many_seats_did_Labour_win-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> The party lost 40 of its 41 seats in Scotland in the face of record swings to the Scottish National Party.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Though Labour gained more than 20 seats in England and Wales, mostly from the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a> but also from the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-irishtimes.com_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-irishtimes.com-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> it lost more seats to the Conservatives, including Ed Balls in <a href="/wiki/Morley_and_Outwood_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Morley and Outwood (UK Parliament constituency)">Morley and Outwood</a>, for net losses overall.<sup id="cite_ref-edresigns_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-edresigns-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Realign_and_in_opposition.2C_2015.E2.80.93present"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Realign_and_in_opposition,_2015–present">Realign and in opposition, 2015–present</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_leadership_of_Jeremy_Corbyn" title="Labour Party leadership of Jeremy Corbyn">Labour Party leadership of Jeremy Corbyn</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Official_portrait_of_Jeremy_Corbyn_crop_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Official_portrait_of_Jeremy_Corbyn_crop_2.jpg/170px-Official_portrait_of_Jeremy_Corbyn_crop_2.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Official_portrait_of_Jeremy_Corbyn_crop_2.jpg/255px-Official_portrait_of_Jeremy_Corbyn_crop_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Official_portrait_of_Jeremy_Corbyn_crop_2.jpg/340px-Official_portrait_of_Jeremy_Corbyn_crop_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1140" data-file-height="1520" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Official_portrait_of_Jeremy_Corbyn_crop_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Corbyn" title="Jeremy Corbyn">Jeremy Corbyn</a>, current leader of the party.</div></div></div>
<p>The day after 7 May 2015 election, Miliband resigned as party leader. Harriet Harman again became acting leader.<sup id="cite_ref-edresigns_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-edresigns-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> The Labour Party held a <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_2015" title="Labour Party (UK) leadership election, 2015">leadership election</a>, in which <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Corbyn" title="Jeremy Corbyn">Jeremy Corbyn</a>, then a member of the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Campaign_Group" title="Socialist Campaign Group">Socialist Campaign Group</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Mason_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mason-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> was considered a fringe hopeful when the contest began, receiving nominations from just 36 MPs, one more than the minimum required to stand, and the support of just 16 MPs.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> However, he benefited from a large influx of new members as well as new affiliated and registered supporters introduced under Miliband.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc-20150812_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-20150812-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> On 12 September, he was elected leader by a landslide. Membership numbers continued to climb after the start of Corbyn's leadership.<sup id="cite_ref-ibtimes_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ibtimes-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Tensions soon developed in the parliamentary party over Corbyn's leadership. Following the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum,_2016" title="United Kingdom European Union membership referendum, 2016">referendum on EU membership</a> more than two dozen members of the <a href="/wiki/Shadow_Cabinet_of_Jeremy_Corbyn" title="Shadow Cabinet of Jeremy Corbyn">Shadow Cabinet</a> resigned in late June 2016,<sup id="cite_ref-guardian-20160627_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-20160627-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> and a no-confidence vote was supported by 172 MPs against 40 supporting Corbyn.<sup id="cite_ref-Elgot_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elgot-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> On 11 July 2016 <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_2016" title="Labour Party (UK) leadership election, 2016">an official leadership election</a> was called as <a href="/wiki/Angela_Eagle" title="Angela Eagle">Angela Eagle</a> launched a challenge against Corbyn.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc-20160711_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-20160711-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> She was soon joined by rival challenger <a href="/wiki/Owen_Smith" title="Owen Smith">Owen Smith</a>, prompting Eagle to withdraw on 19 July 2016 in order to ensure there was only one challenger on the ballot.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> On 24 September 2016 Corbyn retained leadership of the party with an increased share of the vote.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC240916_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC240916-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> By the end of the contest Labour's membership had grown to more than 500,000, making it the largest political party in terms of membership in Western Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the party's decision to support the <a href="/wiki/European_Union_(Notification_of_Withdrawal)_Bill_2017" class="mw-redirect" title="European Union (Notification of Withdrawal) Bill 2017">European Union (Notification of Withdrawal) Bill 2017</a>, at least three shadow cabinet ministers, all representing constituencies which voted to remain in the EU, resigned from their position as a result of the party's decision to invoke <a href="/wiki/Article_50" class="mw-redirect" title="Article 50">Article 50</a> under the bill.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> 47 of 229 Labour MPs voted against the bill (in defiance of the party's <a href="/wiki/Three-line_whip" class="mw-redirect" title="Three-line whip">three-line whip</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Unusually, the rebel frontbenchers did not face immediate dismissal.<sup id="cite_ref-MSavageTimes.1_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MSavageTimes.1-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> According to the <i><a href="/wiki/New_Statesman" title="New Statesman">New Statesman</a></i>, approximately 7,000 members of the Labour Party also resigned in protest over the party's stance;<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> this number has been confirmed by senior Labour sources.<sup id="cite_ref-MSavageTimes.1_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MSavageTimes.1-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 18 April 2017, the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a> announced she would seek an unexpected <a href="/wiki/Snap_election" title="Snap election">snap election</a> on 8 June 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Corbyn said he welcomed May's proposal and said his party would support the government's move in the parliamentary vote announced for 19 April.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The necessary <a href="/wiki/Supermajority" title="Supermajority">super-majority of two-thirds</a> was achieved when 522 of the <a href="/wiki/Number_of_Westminster_MPs" title="Number of Westminster MPs">650 Members of Parliament</a> voted in support.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Some of the <a href="/wiki/Opinion_polling_for_the_United_Kingdom_general_election,_2017" title="Opinion polling for the United Kingdom general election, 2017">opinion polls</a> had shown a 20-point Conservative lead over Labour before the election was called, but this lead had narrowed by the day of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2017" title="United Kingdom general election, 2017">2017 general election</a>, which resulted in a <a href="/wiki/Hung_parliament" title="Hung parliament">hung parliament</a>. Despite remaining in opposition for its third election in a row, Labour at 40.0% won its greatest share of the vote since <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2001" title="United Kingdom general election, 2001">2001</a>, made a net gain of 30 seats to reach 262 total MPs, and, with a swing of 9.6%,<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> achieved the biggest percentage-point increase in its vote share in a single general election since <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1945" title="United Kingdom general election, 1945">1945</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-londoneconomic_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-londoneconomic-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Immediately following the election party membership rose by 35,000.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>  In July 2017 opinion polling suggested Labour leads the Conservatives, 45% to 39%<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> while a <a href="/wiki/YouGov" title="YouGov">YouGov</a> poll gave Labour an 8-point lead over the Conservatives.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 2017 <a href="/wiki/Loughborough_University" title="Loughborough University">Loughborough University</a>'s Centre for Research in Communication and Culture considered a majority of media reports on Labour to be critical compared to coverage of the Conservatives.<sup id="cite_ref-independent_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-independent-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Daily Mail</i> and <i>Daily Express</i> praised Theresa May for election pledges that were condemned when proposed by Labour in previous elections.<sup id="cite_ref-huffingtonpost_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-huffingtonpost-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2018 polls by <a href="/wiki/YouGov" title="YouGov">YouGov</a> and <a href="/wiki/ICM_Research" title="ICM Research">ICM Research</a> both suggest Labour could make substantial  gains by supporting a second referendum on <a href="/wiki/Brexit" title="Brexit">Brexit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ideology">Ideology</span></h2>
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%"><tbody><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em;background:#B60202;padding:0.3em;color:white;"><a href="/wiki/Social_democracy" title="Social democracy"><span style="background-color: #B60202; color:white;">Social democracy</span></a></th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;padding:0.65em;"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Red_rose_02.svg/100px-Red_rose_02.svg.png" width="100" height="95" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Red_rose_02.svg/150px-Red_rose_02.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Red_rose_02.svg/200px-Red_rose_02.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="611" data-file-height="579" /></td></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">

<li><a href="/wiki/Reformist_Left" title="Reformist Left">Reformist Left</a></li></ul>
</div></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">

</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">
<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;border-top:1px solid red;background:transparent;padding-left:0.3em;text-align:center;color:#B60202;">Organizations</div><div class="NavContent plainlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center;margin-top:0;padding:0 0.6em 0.6em;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/List_of_social_democratic_parties" title="List of social democratic parties">Social democratic parties</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Socialist_International" title="Socialist International">Socialist International</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Socialist_Youth" title="International Union of Socialist Youth">International Union of Socialist Youth</a></li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Progressive_Alliance_(political_international)" class="mw-redirect" title="Progressive Alliance (political international)">Progressive Alliance</a></li>

<li><a href="/wiki/International_Trade_Union_Confederation" title="International Trade Union Confederation">International Trade Union Confederation</a></li></ul></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="plainlist" style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;font-weight:normal;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Portal:Business_and_economics" title="Portal:Business and economics">Economics portal</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Portal:Politics" title="Portal:Politics">Politics portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;border-top:1px solid red;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Social_democracy_sidebar" title="Template:Social democracy sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Social_democracy_sidebar" title="Template talk:Social democracy sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Social_democracy_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The Labour Party is considered to be <a href="/wiki/Centre-left_politics" title="Centre-left politics">left of centre</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> It was initially formed as a means for the <a href="/wiki/Trade_union_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Trade union movement">trade union movement</a> to establish political representation for itself at <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">Westminster</a>. It only gained a "socialist" commitment with the original party constitution of 1918. That "socialist" element, the original <a href="/wiki/Clause_IV" title="Clause IV">Clause IV</a>, was seen by its strongest advocates as a straightforward commitment to the "common ownership", or <a href="/wiki/Nationalization" title="Nationalization">nationalisation</a>, of the "means of production, distribution and exchange". Although about a third of British industry was taken into public ownership after the Second World War, and remained so until the 1980s, the right of the party were questioning the validity of expanding on this objective by the late 1950s. Influenced by <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Crosland" title="Anthony Crosland">Anthony Crosland</a>'s book, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Future_of_Socialism" title="The Future of Socialism">The Future of Socialism</a></i> (1956), the circle around party leader <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Gaitskell" title="Hugh Gaitskell">Hugh Gaitskell</a> felt that the commitment was no longer necessary. While an attempt to remove Clause IV from the party constitution in 1959 failed, <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a>, and the "modernisers" saw the issue as putting off potential voters,<sup id="cite_ref-historytoday.com_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historytoday.com-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> and were successful thirty-five years later,<sup id="cite_ref-Philip_Gould_2011,_p.30_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Philip_Gould_2011,_p.30-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> with only limited opposition from senior figures in the party.<sup id="cite_ref-independent.co.uk_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-independent.co.uk-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25%;&#32;;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>
<div class="quotebox-title" style=""><a href="/wiki/Clause_IV" title="Clause IV">Clause IV</a> (1995)</div>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">The Labour Party is a <a href="/wiki/Democratic_socialism" title="Democratic socialism">democratic socialist</a> party. It believes that by the strength of our common endeavour we achieve more than we achieve alone, so as to create for each of us the means to realise our true potential and for all of us a community in which power, wealth and opportunity are in the hands of the many, not the few, where the rights we enjoy reflect the duties we owe, and where we live together, freely, in a spirit of solidarity, tolerance and respect.</div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">Party Constitution, <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_Rule_Book" title="Labour Party Rule Book">Labour Party Rule Book</a><sup id="cite_ref-constitution_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-constitution-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div><p><a href="/wiki/List_of_UK_Labour_Party_general_election_manifestos" class="mw-redirect" title="List of UK Labour Party general election manifestos">Party electoral manifestos</a> have not contained the term <i>socialism</i> since 1992. The new version of Clause IV, though affirming a commitment to democratic socialism,<sup id="cite_ref-constitution_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-constitution-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hww_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hww-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> no longer definitely commits the party to public ownership of industry: in its place it advocates "the enterprise of the market and the rigour of competition" along with "high quality public services ... either owned by the public or accountable to them."<sup id="cite_ref-constitution_144-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-constitution-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Historically, influenced by <a href="/wiki/Keynesian_economics" title="Keynesian economics">Keynesian economics</a>, the party favoured <a href="/wiki/Economic_interventionism" title="Economic interventionism">government intervention</a> in the economy, and the <a href="/wiki/Income_redistribution" class="mw-redirect" title="Income redistribution">redistribution</a> of wealth. Taxation was seen as a means to achieve a "major redistribution of wealth and income" in the October 1974 election manifesto.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELund2006111_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELund2006111-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> The party also desired increased rights for workers, and a <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">welfare state</a> including publicly funded healthcare.
</p><p>From the late-1980s onwards, the party adopted <a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">free market</a> policies,<sup id="cite_ref-mulholland1_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mulholland1-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> leading many observers to describe the Labour Party as <a href="/wiki/Social_democracy" title="Social democracy">social democratic</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Third_Way" title="Third Way">Third Way</a>, rather than democratic socialist.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHay2002114–115HopkinWincott2006Jessop2004McAnulla2006118,_127,_133,_141MerkelPetringHenkesEgle20084,_25–26,_40,_66_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHay2002114–115HopkinWincott2006Jessop2004McAnulla2006118,_127,_133,_141MerkelPetringHenkesEgle20084,_25–26,_40,_66-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Other commentators go further and argue that traditional social democratic parties across Europe, including the British Labour Party, have been so deeply transformed in recent years that it is no longer possible to describe them ideologically as "social democratic",<sup id="cite_ref-Lavelle_2008_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lavelle_2008-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> and claim that this ideological shift has put new strains on the Labour Party's traditional relationship with the trade unions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDanielsMcIlroy2009McIlroy2011Smith2009SmithMorton2006_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDanielsMcIlroy2009McIlroy2011Smith2009SmithMorton2006-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
Historically within the party, differentiation was made between the <a href="/wiki/Social_democratic" class="mw-redirect" title="Social democratic">social democratic</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Socialist" class="mw-redirect" title="Socialist">socialist</a> wings of the party, the latter often subscribed to a radical socialist, even <a href="/wiki/Marxist" class="mw-redirect" title="Marxist">Marxist</a>, ideology.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrines2011161_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrines2011161-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-What&#39;s_left_of_the_Labour_left_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-What&#39;s_left_of_the_Labour_left-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In more recent times, a limited number of Members of Parliament in the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Campaign_Group" title="Socialist Campaign Group">Socialist Campaign Group</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Representation_Committee_(2004)" title="Labour Representation Committee (2004)">Labour Representation Committee</a> have seen themselves as the standard bearers for the radical socialist tradition in contrast to the democratic socialist tradition represented by organisations such as <i><a href="/wiki/Compass_(think_tank)" title="Compass (think tank)">Compass</a></i> and the magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Tribune_(magazine)" title="Tribune (magazine)">Tribune</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-labourlist.org_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-labourlist.org-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> The group <a href="/wiki/Progress_(organisation)" title="Progress (organisation)">Progress</a>, founded in 1996, represents the centrist/Blairite position in the party and is opposed to the Corbyn leadership.<sup id="cite_ref-progress-20170302_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-progress-20170302-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-progress-20170720_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-progress-20170720-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015, <a href="/wiki/Momentum_(organisation)" title="Momentum (organisation)">Momentum</a> was created by <a href="/wiki/Jon_Lansman" title="Jon Lansman">Jon Lansman</a> as a grass-roots left-wing organisation following <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Corbyn" title="Jeremy Corbyn">Jeremy Corbyn</a>'s election as party leader. Rather than organising among the <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_Labour_Party" title="Parliamentary Labour Party">PLP</a>, Momentum is a rank and file grouping with an estimated 40,000 members.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbols">Symbols</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Red_flag.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Red_flag.svg/130px-Red_flag.svg.png" width="130" height="78" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Red_flag.svg/195px-Red_flag.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Red_flag.svg/260px-Red_flag.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Red_flag.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Red_flag_(politics)" title="Red flag (politics)">red flag</a>, originally the official flag and symbol of the Labour party</div></div></div>
<p>Labour has long been identified with red, a <a href="/wiki/Political_colour" title="Political colour">political colour</a> traditionally affiliated with socialism and the <a href="/wiki/Labour_movement" title="Labour movement">labour movement</a>. Prior to the red flag logo, the party had used a modified version of the classic 1924 shovel, torch and quill emblem. In 1924 a brand conscious Labour leadership had devised a competition, inviting supporters to design a logo to replace the 'polo mint' like motif that had previously appeared on party literature. The winning entry, emblazoned with the word ‘Liberty’ over a design incorporating a torch, shovel and quill symbol, was popularised through its sale, in badge form, for a shilling.
The party conference in 1931 passed a motion "That this conference adopts Party Colours, which should be uniform throughout the country, colours to be red and gold".<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Since the party's inception, the <a href="/wiki/Red_flag_(politics)" title="Red flag (politics)">red flag</a> has been Labour's official symbol; the flag has been associated with socialism and revolution ever since the 1789 <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1848" title="Revolutions of 1848">revolutions of 1848</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Rose" title="Rose">red rose</a>, a symbol of social democracy, was adopted as the party symbol in 1986 as part of a rebranding exercise and is now incorporated into the party logo.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Telegraph_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Telegraph-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The red flag became an inspiration which resulted in the composition of "<a href="/wiki/The_Red_Flag" title="The Red Flag">The Red Flag</a>", the official party anthem since its inception, being sung at the end of party conferences and on various occasions such as in parliament on February 2006 to mark the centenary of the Labour Party's founding. During New Labour attempts were made to play down the role of the song,<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hoggart_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoggart-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> however it still remains in use. The patriotic song <a href="/wiki/And_did_those_feet_in_ancient_time" title="And did those feet in ancient time">Jerusalem</a>, based on a <a href="/wiki/William_Blake" title="William Blake">William Blake</a> poem, is also frequently sung.<sup id="cite_ref-Telegraph.co.uk_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph.co.uk-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Constitution_and_structure">Constitution and structure</span></h2>
<p>The Labour Party is a membership organisation consisting of individual members and <a href="/wiki/Constituency_Labour_Parties" class="mw-redirect" title="Constituency Labour Parties">Constituency Labour Parties</a>, <a href="/wiki/Affiliated_trade_unions" class="mw-redirect" title="Affiliated trade unions">affiliated trade unions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Socialist_societies" class="mw-redirect" title="Socialist societies">socialist societies</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Co-operative_Party" title="Co-operative Party">Co-operative Party</a>, with which it has an electoral agreement. Members who are elected to parliamentary positions take part in the <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_Labour_Party" title="Parliamentary Labour Party">Parliamentary Labour Party</a> (PLP) and <a href="/wiki/European_Parliamentary_Labour_Party" title="European Parliamentary Labour Party">European Parliamentary Labour Party</a> (EPLP).
</p><p>The party's decision-making bodies on a national level formally include the <a href="/wiki/National_Executive_Committee" title="National Executive Committee">National Executive Committee</a> (NEC), <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_Conference" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour Party Conference">Labour Party Conference</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Policy_Forum" title="National Policy Forum">National Policy Forum</a> (NPF)—although in practice the Parliamentary leadership has the final say on policy. The 2008 Labour Party Conference was the first at which affiliated trade unions and Constituency Labour Parties did not have the right to submit motions on contemporary issues that would previously have been debated.<sup id="cite_ref-Anger_over_&#39;union_debate_limit&#39;_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anger_over_&#39;union_debate_limit&#39;-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Labour Party conferences now include more "keynote" addresses, guest speakers and question-and-answer sessions, while specific discussion of policy now takes place in the National Policy Forum.
</p><p>The Labour Party is an <a href="/wiki/Unincorporated_associations_in_English_law" title="Unincorporated associations in English law">unincorporated association</a> without a <a href="/wiki/Legal_person" title="Legal person">separate legal personality</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_Rule_Book" title="Labour Party Rule Book">Labour Party Rule Book</a> legally regulates the organisation and the relationship with members.<sup id="cite_ref-lgn-20150917_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lgn-20150917-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/General_Secretary_of_the_Labour_Party" title="General Secretary of the Labour Party">General Secretary</a> represents the party on behalf of the other members of the Labour Party in any legal matters or actions.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Membership_and_registered_supporters">Membership and registered supporters</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Labour_Party_membership_graph.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Labour_Party_membership_graph.svg/330px-Labour_Party_membership_graph.svg.png" width="330" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Labour_Party_membership_graph.svg/495px-Labour_Party_membership_graph.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Labour_Party_membership_graph.svg/660px-Labour_Party_membership_graph.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Labour_Party_membership_graph.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A graph showing Labour Party individual membership, excluding affiliated members and supporters, 1928 to 2018</div></div></div>
<p>In August 2015, prior to the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_2015" title="Labour Party (UK) leadership election, 2015">2015 leadership election</a>, the Labour Party reported 292,505 full members, 147,134 affiliated supporters (mostly from affiliated <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">trade unions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Socialist_society_(Labour_Party)" title="Socialist society (Labour Party)">socialist societies</a>) and 110,827 registered supporters; a total of about 550,000 members and supporters.<sup id="cite_ref-independent-20150910_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-independent-20150910-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mirror-20150825_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mirror-20150825-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> As of  December 2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Labour_Party_(UK)&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the party had approximately 552,000 full members, making it the largest political party in Western Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-huffpost-20170613_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-huffpost-20170613-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Consequently membership fees became the largest component of the party's income, overtaking trade unions donations which were previously of most financial importance, and in 2017 making Labour the most financially well-off British political party.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian-20180822_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-20180822-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For many years Labour held to a policy of not allowing residents of <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> to apply for membership,<sup id="cite_ref-labour.org.uk_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-labour.org.uk-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> instead supporting the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_and_Labour_Party" title="Social Democratic and Labour Party">Social Democratic and Labour Party</a> (SDLP) which informally takes the Labour whip in the House of Commons.<sup id="cite_ref-Understanding_Ulster_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Understanding_Ulster-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> The 2003 Labour Party Conference accepted legal advice that the party could not continue to prohibit residents of the province joining,<sup id="cite_ref-Labour_NI_ban_overturned_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Labour_NI_ban_overturned-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> and whilst the National Executive has established a regional constituency party it has not yet agreed to contest elections there. In December 2015 a meeting of the members of the Labour Party in Northern Ireland decided unanimously to contest the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly_election,_2016" title="Northern Ireland Assembly election, 2016">elections for the Northern Ireland Assembly held in May 2016</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade_union_link">Trade union link</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Trade_unionism_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Trade unionism in the United Kingdom">Trade unionism in the United Kingdom</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Unite_the_Union_decked_out_for_the_2015_General_Election_on_Call_Lane,_Leeds_(3rd_May_2015)_001.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Unite_the_Union_decked_out_for_the_2015_General_Election_on_Call_Lane%2C_Leeds_%283rd_May_2015%29_001.JPG/180px-Unite_the_Union_decked_out_for_the_2015_General_Election_on_Call_Lane%2C_Leeds_%283rd_May_2015%29_001.JPG" width="180" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Unite_the_Union_decked_out_for_the_2015_General_Election_on_Call_Lane%2C_Leeds_%283rd_May_2015%29_001.JPG/270px-Unite_the_Union_decked_out_for_the_2015_General_Election_on_Call_Lane%2C_Leeds_%283rd_May_2015%29_001.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Unite_the_Union_decked_out_for_the_2015_General_Election_on_Call_Lane%2C_Leeds_%283rd_May_2015%29_001.JPG/360px-Unite_the_Union_decked_out_for_the_2015_General_Election_on_Call_Lane%2C_Leeds_%283rd_May_2015%29_001.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Unite_the_Union_decked_out_for_the_2015_General_Election_on_Call_Lane,_Leeds_(3rd_May_2015)_001.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Unite_the_Union" title="Unite the Union">Unite the Union</a> showing their support for the Labour party on their <a href="/wiki/Leeds" title="Leeds">Leeds</a> offices during the 2015 general election</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Trade_Union_and_Labour_Party_Liaison_Organisation" title="Trade Union and Labour Party Liaison Organisation">Trade Union and Labour Party Liaison Organisation</a> is the co-ordinating structure that supports the policy and campaign activities of affiliated union members within the Labour Party at the national, regional and local level.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As it was founded by the unions to represent the interests of working-class people, Labour's link with the unions has always been a defining characteristic of the party. In recent years this link has come under increasing strain, with the <a href="/wiki/National_Union_of_Rail,_Maritime_and_Transport_Workers" title="National Union of Rail, Maritime and Transport Workers">RMT</a> being expelled from the party in 2004 for allowing its branches in Scotland to affiliate to the left-wing <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Socialist_Party" title="Scottish Socialist Party">Scottish Socialist Party</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RMT_&#39;breached&#39;_Labour_party_rules_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RMT_&#39;breached&#39;_Labour_party_rules-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Other unions have also faced calls from members to reduce financial support for the Party<sup id="cite_ref-Labour&#39;s_link_to_unions_in_danger_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Labour&#39;s_link_to_unions_in_danger-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> and seek more effective political representation for their views on <a href="/wiki/Privatisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Privatisation">privatisation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Public_spending" class="mw-redirect" title="Public spending">public spending</a> cuts and the anti-<a href="/wiki/Trade_unions_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Trade unions in the United Kingdom">trade union</a> laws.<sup id="cite_ref-TUC_Congress_Voices_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TUC_Congress_Voices-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> Unison and <a href="/wiki/GMB_(trade_union)" title="GMB (trade union)">GMB</a> have both threatened to withdraw funding from constituency MPs and Dave Prentis of <a href="/wiki/UNISON" title="UNISON">UNISON</a> has warned that the union will write "no more blank cheques" and is dissatisfied with "feeding the hand that bites us".<sup id="cite_ref-Dunton_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dunton-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> Union funding was redesigned in 2013 after the <a href="/wiki/2013_Labour_Party_Falkirk_candidate_selection" title="2013 Labour Party Falkirk candidate selection">Falkirk candidate-selection controversy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_News_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Fire_Brigades_Union" title="Fire Brigades Union">Fire Brigades Union</a>, which "severed links" with Labour in 2004, re-joined the party under Corbyn's leadership in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_and_international_affiliation">European and international affiliation</span></h3>
<p>The Labour Party is a founder member of the <a href="/wiki/Party_of_European_Socialists" title="Party of European Socialists">Party of European Socialists</a> (PES). The European Parliamentary Labour Party's 20 <a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_European_Parliament" title="Member of the European Parliament">MEPs</a> are part of the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Alliance_of_Socialists_and_Democrats" title="Progressive Alliance of Socialists and Democrats">Socialists and Democrats (S&amp;D)</a>, the second largest <a href="/wiki/Political_groups_of_the_European_Parliament" title="Political groups of the European Parliament">group in the European Parliament</a>. The Labour Party is represented by <a href="/wiki/Emma_Reynolds" title="Emma Reynolds">Emma Reynolds</a> in the PES Presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The party was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Labour_and_Socialist_International" title="Labour and Socialist International">Labour and Socialist International</a> between 1923 and 1940.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> Since 1951 the party has been a member of the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_International" title="Socialist International">Socialist International</a>, which was founded thanks to the efforts of the Clement Attlee leadership. However, in February 2013, the Labour Party NEC decided to downgrade participation to observer membership status, "in view of ethical concerns, and to develop international co-operation through new networks".<sup id="cite_ref-Black_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Black-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Labour was a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Alliance" title="Progressive Alliance">Progressive Alliance</a> international founded in co-operation with the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_of_Germany" title="Social Democratic Party of Germany">Social Democratic Party of Germany</a> and other social-democratic parties on 22 May 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-Spiegel.de_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spiegel.de-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vorwurf:_SPD_spaltet_die_Linken_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vorwurf:_SPD_spaltet_die_Linken-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Morgenweb.de_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morgenweb.de-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<dd></dd></dl>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-187"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-187">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The first election held under the <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1918" title="Representation of the People Act 1918">Representation of the People Act 1918</a> in which all men over 21, and most women over the age of 30 could vote, and therefore a much larger electorate.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-188"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-188">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The first election held under the <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_(Equal_Franchise)_Act_1928" title="Representation of the People (Equal Franchise) Act 1928">Representation of the People Act 1928</a> which gave all women aged over 21 the vote.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-189"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-189">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Franchise extended to all 18- to 20-year-olds under the <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1969" title="Representation of the People Act 1969">Representation of the People Act 1969</a>.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Parliament_elections">European Parliament elections</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elections_to_the_European_Parliament" title="Elections to the European Parliament">Elections to the European Parliament</a></div>
<p>Elections to the European Parliament began in 1979, and were held under the <a href="/wiki/First-past-the-post_voting" title="First-past-the-post voting">first past the post</a> system until 1999, when a form of <a href="/wiki/Proportional_representation" title="Proportional representation">proportional representation</a> was introduced.
</p>

</th>

</th>

</th>

</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_1979_(United_Kingdom)" title="European Parliament election, 1979 (United Kingdom)">1979</a>
</td>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership">Leadership</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leaders_of_the_Labour_Party_since_1906">Leaders of the Labour Party since 1906</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Leader of the Labour Party (UK)">Leader of the Labour Party (UK)</a></div>
<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 20em; -webkit-column-width: 20em; column-width: 20em;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Keir_Hardie" title="Keir Hardie">Keir Hardie</a>, 1906–08</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arthur_Henderson" title="Arthur Henderson">Arthur Henderson</a>, 1908–10</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/George_Nicoll_Barnes" class="mw-redirect" title="George Nicoll Barnes">George Nicoll Barnes</a>, 1910–11</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ramsay_MacDonald" title="Ramsay MacDonald">Ramsay MacDonald</a>, 1911–14</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arthur_Henderson" title="Arthur Henderson">Arthur Henderson</a>, 1914–17</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/William_Adamson" title="William Adamson">William Adamson</a>, 1917–21</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_Robert_Clynes" class="mw-redirect" title="John Robert Clynes">John Robert Clynes</a>, 1921–22</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ramsay_MacDonald" title="Ramsay MacDonald">Ramsay MacDonald</a>, 1922–31</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arthur_Henderson" title="Arthur Henderson">Arthur Henderson</a>, 1931–32</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/George_Lansbury" title="George Lansbury">George Lansbury</a>, 1932–35</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Clement_Attlee" title="Clement Attlee">Clement Attlee</a>, 1935–55</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hugh_Gaitskell" title="Hugh Gaitskell">Hugh Gaitskell</a>, 1955–63
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/George_Brown,_Baron_George-Brown" title="George Brown, Baron George-Brown">George Brown</a>, 1963 (acting)</li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a>, 1963–76</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a>, 1976–80</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Michael_Foot" title="Michael Foot">Michael Foot</a>, 1980–83</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Neil_Kinnock" title="Neil Kinnock">Neil Kinnock</a>, 1983–92</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_Smith_(Labour_Party_leader)" title="John Smith (Labour Party leader)">John Smith</a>, 1992–94
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Margaret_Beckett" title="Margaret Beckett">Margaret Beckett</a>, 1994 (acting)<sup id="cite_ref-rule_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rule-191">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a>, 1994–2007</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a>, 2007–2010
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Harriet_Harman" title="Harriet Harman">Harriet Harman</a>, 2010 (acting)<sup id="cite_ref-rule_191-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rule-191">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ed_Miliband" title="Ed Miliband">Ed Miliband</a>, 2010–2015
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Harriet_Harman" title="Harriet Harman">Harriet Harman</a>, 2015 (acting)</li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Corbyn" title="Jeremy Corbyn">Jeremy Corbyn</a>, 2015–present</li></ul>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Living_former_Labour_Party_leaders">Living former Labour Party leaders</span></h3>
